  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1a974b4a-82a7-4d48-a95b-3c42074df650'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17356729424252858200">A chance meeting on the Fijian island of Taveuni is the trigger for a fascinating and mysterious novel that intertwines the stories of John Spooke, an English author who is grieving for his dead wife; Frank Andersen, a Norwegian evolutionary biologist estranged from his wife Vera; and an enigmatic Spanish couple, Ana and Jose, who are absorbed in their love for each other.</span>
  <span id="freeText17356729424252858200" style="display:none">A chance meeting on the Fijian island of Taveuni is the trigger for a fascinating and mysterious novel that intertwines the stories of John Spooke, an English author who is grieving for his dead wife; Frank Andersen, a Norwegian evolutionary biologist estranged from his wife Vera; and an enigmatic Spanish couple, Ana and Jose, who are absorbed in their love for each other. Why does Ana bear such a close resemblance to the model for Goya's famous Maja paintings? What is the significance of the Joker as he steps out of his pack of cards? As the action moves from Fiji to Spain, from the present to the past, unfolding further stories within the stories, the novel reveals an astonishing richness and complexity. As bold and imaginative in its sweep as Sophie's World, it shows again that Jostein Gaarder's unique and special gift is to make us wonder at the awe-inspiring mystery of the universe.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17356729424252858200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer368223724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1194816174780415651"><b>Maya, Jostein Gaarder</b><br/><br/>A chance meeting on the Fijian island of Taveuni is the trigger for a fascinating and mysterious novel that intertwines the stories of John Spooke, an English author who is grieving for his dead wife; Frank Andersen, a Norwegian evolutionary biologist estranged from his wife Vera; and an enigmatic Spanish couple, Ana and Jose, who are absorbed in their love for each other. <br/><br/>Why does Ana bear such a close resemblance to the model for Goya's famous Maja paintings? <br/><br/>What is the s</span>
  <span id="freeText1194816174780415651" style="display:none"><b>Maya, Jostein Gaarder</b><br /><br />A chance meeting on the Fijian island of Taveuni is the trigger for a fascinating and mysterious novel that intertwines the stories of John Spooke, an English author who is grieving for his dead wife; Frank Andersen, a Norwegian evolutionary biologist estranged from his wife Vera; and an enigmatic Spanish couple, Ana and Jose, who are absorbed in their love for each other. <br /><br />Why does Ana bear such a close resemblance to the model for Goya's famous Maja paintings? <br /><br />What is the significance of the Joker as he steps out of his pack of cards? <br /><br />As the action moves from Fiji to Spain, from the present to the past, unfolding further stories within the stories, the novel reveals an astonishing richness and complexity. As bold and imaginative in its sweep as Sophie's World, it shows again that Jostein Gaarder's unique and special gift is to make us wonder at the awe-inspiring mystery of the universe.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز هفدهم ماه ژوئن سال 2012میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: مایا؛ نویسنده: یوستین(یاستین) گوردر؛ مترجم: مهرداد بازیاریی؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، هرمس، 1387، در 406ص، شابک 9789643633431؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان نروژی سده 20م<br /><br />داستان سه روز زندگی: «یک زیست شناس تنهای نروژی»، «یک رمان نویس انگلیسی»، و «یک زوج جوان اسپانیایی» در جزیره ی همانند بهشت «تاوئونی» است؛ گذراندن شبهای طولانی با «بریج»، «قصه گفتن» و «اندیشیدن» است؛ «فرانک» و «ورا» پس از درگذشت دخترشان «سوفیا» از یکدیگر جدا شدند، و اکنون «فرانک» نامه ای به «ورا مینویسد» تا شاید با نامه او را به بازگشت قانع نماید؛ او در این نامه شرح سفر خود، به جزایر گرمسیری و دیدارش با زن و شوهری عجیب به نام «خوزه» و «آنا» را مینمایاند، از دوزیستان و تکاملشان سخن آغاز میکند و به بحثهای آفرینش فلسفی میرسد<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 14/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="1194816174780415651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151135028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12874192961075416199">This is the second novel I've read by Gaarder. As an admirer of philosophy, it's not as informative as Sophie's World, but they both manifest Gaarder as a wise writer. This story speaks of love, loss, and meaning. It touches on philosophy and evolution. I like Gaarder's strong non-religious philosophical faith in God which makes an atheist's stance looks ridiculous. As he says, in terms of probability, nothingness would've been far more likely, and I agree. I also like the idea presented in the </span>
  <span id="freeText12874192961075416199" style="display:none">This is the second novel I've read by Gaarder. As an admirer of philosophy, it's not as informative as Sophie's World, but they both manifest Gaarder as a wise writer. This story speaks of love, loss, and meaning. It touches on philosophy and evolution. I like Gaarder's strong non-religious philosophical faith in God which makes an atheist's stance looks ridiculous. As he says, in terms of probability, nothingness would've been far more likely, and I agree. I also like the idea presented in the story of purposeful evolution. It was quite interesting. Castle in the Pyrenees will be my next station with Gaarder. 4 stars for Maya if it didn't have many long tedious details at some points. </span>

After garnishing with a little bit of mystery, he tops it with some romance.   <span id="freeText11807045335201561801" style="display:none">Jostein Gaarder prepares a cake called "Maya" by mixing Indian Advaita Philosophy with Darwin's theory of Evolution and baking it in an oven at a temperature of intellectual stimulation. After garnishing with a little bit of mystery, he tops it with some romance. The cake is served in a shape of a very very long letter. <br />Unfortunately, the resultant is so unpalatable that even if you could enjoy the flavour, you don't really like the overall treat. Especially when the chef had earlier given you "Sophie's World", one of the best dishes you had ever had. <br /><br />Loved these lines the best:<br /><blockquote><br />	We bear and are borne by a soul we do not know. <br />	When the riddle raises itself on two legs without being solved, it is our turn.<br />	When the dream pinches its own arm without waking, it is us.<br />	For we are the riddle no one guesses. <br />	We are the fairy tale trapped in its own image.<br />	We are that moves on and on without arriving at understanding…..<br /></blockquote></span>

          </span>

                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3148380843531999172">like every book by jostein gaarder (one of my all-time favorite authors), "maya" was beautiful and nostalgic. it lectures about life and death in a way that seems poetic enough to deal with and absorb positively. but i found myself, after having read so many jostein gaarder books, that this one did not flow with the ease and rhythmic passion the others have. it felt rather long and droning. i did not enjoy the endless conversations between frank and gordon the gecko, nor the exchanges between fr</span>
  <span id="freeText3148380843531999172" style="display:none">like every book by jostein gaarder (one of my all-time favorite authors), "maya" was beautiful and nostalgic. it lectures about life and death in a way that seems poetic enough to deal with and absorb positively. but i found myself, after having read so many jostein gaarder books, that this one did not flow with the ease and rhythmic passion the others have. it felt rather long and droning. i did not enjoy the endless conversations between frank and gordon the gecko, nor the exchanges between frank and josé, or frank and john. in fact, john, the plotting matchmaking widower, who features prominently only at the beginning and at the end of the book, felt more relatable to me than frank, whom we follow through the entire book. he was too defeated, and i hated how defeated i felt myself when i read about him, except maybe when he went into his interesting, although extensive, lectures about the big bang and the evolution of the species. <br />i think my favorite character was laura, the avid reader of "lonely planet", because i don't know quite how to feel about her; she's ambiguous to me, whereas all the other characters either unnerved me or mildly interested me.<br />the part i enjoyed the most was when, after hearing so many dispersed quotes from the manifesto that reminded me so much of another gaarder book, the connection was finally revealed with "the solitaire mystery", my absolute favorite book by jostein gaarder, and that alignment of the two stories made me immensely happy, although it wasn't a long-lasting feeling. but the manifesto itself felt too much like a copycat of the tiny book from "the solitaire mystery", which revealed to hans thomas things about the past, present and future. <br />there were some very poetic instances throughout the book, and i've found myself writing down some quotes which i found solemnly beautiful. the ending was nice, albeit rather predictable, and it gave me a sensation of fulfillment, just not as strong and reverberating as previous jostein gaarder books. maybe my expectations were too high, i needed this book to surpass my love for "the solitaire mystery" or "sophy's world", but it didn't quite get there.<br />but i've found i quite like the name maya now, for all it's implications and meanings. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3148380843531999172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_80082371" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80082371" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80082371?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80082371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer507808256426127567">This is really the first "philosophy" book I've read, and I'm not sure what to make of it, honestly. The initial part of this book, in which the background hints that there is a bit of a mystery are given, to me had a little bit of a gothic mystery feel. Everything seems normal on top, but underneath, there's something just not quite right. It had me intrigued, and was enough of an intrigue to allow me to read through this book in the better part of a day and a half, despite some very deep intel</span>
  <span id="freeText507808256426127567" style="display:none">This is really the first "philosophy" book I've read, and I'm not sure what to make of it, honestly. The initial part of this book, in which the background hints that there is a bit of a mystery are given, to me had a little bit of a gothic mystery feel. Everything seems normal on top, but underneath, there's something just not quite right. It had me intrigued, and was enough of an intrigue to allow me to read through this book in the better part of a day and a half, despite some very deep intellectual and spiritual content. <br /><br />I really did enjoy that content, which surprised me as I am not drawn to philosophy in general, so maybe my choice of the word "despite" above is wrong. But that's how it feels. I read quickly for the mystery, but not so quickly that I couldn't grasp and ponder the deeper stuff that the mystery was a part of. <br /><br />I recognized a lot of my own thought processes and neuroses in this book, much to my surprise. Concepts like whether we're really here at all, or if we're just part of a dream that we're not enlightened enough to wake from in order to recognize it for what it is... Concepts like death, and not so much what becomes of the deceased after death, but what happens to the rest of existence after the death of the deceased. I myself find it hard to contemplate that the world, existence as I know it, will continue on in the same way as it always has when I'm gone. Logically, this makes sense, but even as a little girl, I've wondered how things would change if I no longer existed as part of the world. Not if I had never existed, but if I was no longer there to see it. Would my mother still be my mother as I know her? Would my friends still be my friends as I've known them? Or would something shift and be different, but I wouldn't know because I'm not there to see? <br /><br />In a way, one would think that these thoughts should make me believe in a life after death, so that I can still look on the world and keep up with my friends and loved ones... but I see this as two separate things. If I was a religious person, perhaps I wouldn't have this fear, because I'd have faith in something greater than myself. But I'm not religious, and this fear is not currently enough for me to be able to change this. It's not a great enough fear that I need something to soothe it, if that something requires believing in what to me is improbable, if not impossible. <br /><br />I'm kind of rambling on, now, but I just wanted to talk about some of the things that this book made me think as I was reading it. Another thing that kind of struck me about this book is that it seemed to be a kind of meeting of the minds between "Evolutionary spirituality" and "Religious spirituality". It struck me how we're products of millions and millions of years of selective evolution, but that there could be a "creator" out there who got the ball rolling and who is subtly making sure that things take a certain path to a wanted result. Is that result us? Or are we only a step in the path to an end result we can't even fathom yet?<br /><br />Anyway... So I mentioned before that this book started out with a feeling of a gothic mystery, but it quickly changed as the focus became more of the philosophical aspects, and then about 3/4 of the way through, it seemed to morph again into a kind of philosophical magical realism style. Things then started to take a strange and unreal turn, and this is where I think I got a little lost. I don't understand what happened at the end of the book, or how things came together. I'm happy with it, but I don't understand how it came about. <br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed this book, but I'm not sure if philosophy is really my thing. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="507808256426127567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26509198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26509198" />
    <a title="Fajar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1089092-fajar"><img alt="Fajar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208376719p2/1089092.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26509198?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fajar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fajar" href="/user/show/1089092-fajar">Fajar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26509198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9908975567810344314">I had high hopes with this one. After all, Gaarder was the man who penned one of the modern classics that is Sophie’s World. <br/><br/>And he didn’t disappoint me. Maya is definitely a worthy successor to the likes of Sophie’s World and Vita Brevis. Staying true to his calling, he takes us in a tour-de-force of hard philosophical questions that were difficult to imagine, - let alone deliberate, by anyone alone. This, - I think, is where his greatest influence is supposed to be : in his provocations where </span>
  <span id="freeText9908975567810344314" style="display:none">I had high hopes with this one. After all, Gaarder was the man who penned one of the modern classics that is Sophie’s World. <br /><br />And he didn’t disappoint me. Maya is definitely a worthy successor to the likes of Sophie’s World and Vita Brevis. Staying true to his calling, he takes us in a tour-de-force of hard philosophical questions that were difficult to imagine, - let alone deliberate, by anyone alone. This, - I think, is where his greatest influence is supposed to be : in his provocations where one is forced to take a long, hard look at oneself and ponder why is he living at all. Such incitements are necessary because by default, man always strive to fulfil, but not question, his needs.<br /><br />Focusing more to the question of evolution and biology, Maya nevertheless feel a little bit constrained by the love-story plot. This did not become obvious, however, until the last 40 pages or so. A savoring reader thus will not have any problems in immersing into Gaarder’s typical mental discourses happening all over this book.<br /><br />What more interesting is his try to take on mystery / thriller genre, - however slight. Granted, in the end the mystery was only used as a device to build the momentum towards climax; but it’s still intriguing for me that he adopt such an adventurous plan. Not that it didn’t work. On the contrary, the execution was of such excellence that I was fooled into thinking that this is truly an enigmatic book in the same league as Eco. Now that I had time to think about the issue, maybe it shouldn’t be surprising at all. I mean, a man who can construct a very exciting verbal scene full of anguish between his human character and a gecko; will surely be able to keep us in his grip in one or two alternate methods.<br /><br />The epilogue, then, explains all. Only then it became apparent that this is a first and foremost, a love story. A very beautiful and profound love story, for that matter. Yet, I can not help but think that this is also this book’s weak point. Had Gaarder been more flexible; maybe letting go to the momentum and taking on the metaphysical thriller theme to the end will pay more. It almost feels like a hangover for me : riding so high in the buildup, but then take a sharp ( although admittedly sweet ) drop in the end. It then dictates a necessary evaluation of the question : Should a literary work’s end justify the means ?<br /><br />Finally, this book again exhibits Gaarder’s ability as a storyteller. Bearing that small complain in mind, I’d still gladly recommend this one. If you don’t read this book with a critic’s spectacle, then it’s truly enjoyable to let yourself go, even swept by some of the finest dialogues I’ve ever read in modern fiction..!!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9908975567810344314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_275477395" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/275477395" />
    <a title="Sabrina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3327557-sabrina"><img alt="Sabrina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522655142p2/3327557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/275477395?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sabrina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sabrina" href="/user/show/3327557-sabrina">Sabrina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788535900811" href="/book/show/222176.Maya">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer275477395&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer275477395" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8252611318256678186">I have two comments about the book:<br/><br/>1) Reading Maya as a philosophy book is a mistake.<br/>It is a romance that brings important philosophical reflections, and leads you deeper into questioning why we are here and whether or not there is a purpose for everything that exists. But it is a love story, a story of many 'loves', that includes philosophy throughout as it would be expected of Gaarder. If you are looking for a philosophy book, I would recommend something else. <br/><br/>2) It was, for me, a very good bo</span>
  <span id="freeText8252611318256678186" style="display:none">I have two comments about the book:<br /><br />1) Reading Maya as a philosophy book is a mistake.<br />It is a romance that brings important philosophical reflections, and leads you deeper into questioning why we are here and whether or not there is a purpose for everything that exists. But it is a love story, a story of many 'loves', that includes philosophy throughout as it would be expected of Gaarder. If you are looking for a philosophy book, I would recommend something else. <br /><br />2) It was, for me, a very good book, but the timing was wrong.<br />I believe books speak to us differently depending on which context we are in and how life is at that very moment. My moment in life now was certainly not right for Maya. I needed more closure, and the book brings the opposite. At the end, reality and fiction are mixed and cannot be separated. But for this moment in my life I would have preferred to be told which one was 'true', even if Gaarder would argue that the fictional part was true, no matter how fantastic and absurd it may seem. When I got to the end of the book, I was prepared for that. But I was not prepared to have such a tangled ending. <br /><br />It is important to say that this does not make it a bad book. It is certainly a very interesting read and I will try to return to it when the time is right. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8252611318256678186" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_103667910" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103667910" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3220174-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267667734p2/3220174.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103667910?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/3220174-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it

  <span id="freeText1066015854747986006" style="display:none">I can't decide if I liked this book or not. This one didn't capture my attention immediately like I remember from all of the other books by Gaarder I've read. The beginning wasn't all that compelling and I almost gave up on the book a third of the way through (which is quite a bit of slow reading). The first half is philosophical musings and setup for the second half. The second half of the book is the meaty story part -- the mystery, the chase, the love story, the climax and then a sharp turn to the end. The ending is cute, but after chasing along with Frank to his "ending" in his letters to Vera, the postscript stroll by John Spooke just feels a bit unsatisfying. Even though it was many years ago, having read The Solitaire Mystery definitely helped.</span>

<div id="review_595761254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/595761254" />
    <a title="Michael Belcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6222146-michael-belcher"><img alt="Michael Belcher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589825129p2/6222146.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/595761254?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Belcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Belcher" href="/user/show/6222146-michael-belcher">Michael Belcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer595761254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14891036057169244507">What begins as a slow, plodding rumination on not-much-at-all (i.e. repetitive lessons on evolutionary biology and the ramblings of characters you don't yet care about) turns into an intriguing, slow-boiling mystery about life: both backwards and forwards. I found myself riveted once the initial banality dissolved, eagerly speeding through the rest of the book, and by the time I finished the novel even the first 100 pages took on a new, fascinating appeal. It's a bold move for a writer to start </span>
  <span id="freeText14891036057169244507" style="display:none">What begins as a slow, plodding rumination on not-much-at-all (i.e. repetitive lessons on evolutionary biology and the ramblings of characters you don't yet care about) turns into an intriguing, slow-boiling mystery about life: both backwards and forwards. I found myself riveted once the initial banality dissolved, eagerly speeding through the rest of the book, and by the time I finished the novel even the first 100 pages took on a new, fascinating appeal. It's a bold move for a writer to start with confounding prose that will only make sense in retrospect, but the payoff for any vigilant reader is well-worth the price of admission. I just wish the characters themselves were as finely wrought as the fascinating coincidences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14891036057169244507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_161974766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161974766" />
    <a title="May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/255889-may"><img alt="May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514757205p2/255889.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161974766?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="May" href="/user/show/255889-may">May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161974766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer824430706638284334">This is the second Gaarder book I have read, the first being Sophie's World. Other reviews mentioned reading the Solitaire Man was helpful to them, and I wish that I had read it because it would have given the story more meaning. <br/><br/>I enjoy philosophy books, and I loved Sophie's World. I found the evolution speeches by the main character, Frank, very interesting. That's the kind of thing I couldn't read in a textbook, but putting it in a story makes much more sense to me. That being said, there is </span>
  <span id="freeText824430706638284334" style="display:none">This is the second Gaarder book I have read, the first being Sophie's World. Other reviews mentioned reading the Solitaire Man was helpful to them, and I wish that I had read it because it would have given the story more meaning. <br /><br />I enjoy philosophy books, and I loved Sophie's World. I found the evolution speeches by the main character, Frank, very interesting. That's the kind of thing I couldn't read in a textbook, but putting it in a story makes much more sense to me. That being said, there is a large portion of the book where Frank is talking to a gecko who he imagines talks back to him. As a device to write more scientific information it is very creative, but as a plot point I thought it was used too much. I think some of those conversations could have been cut down. <br /><br />I was also confused about one of the major plot points. It seems there is a mystery, and that was the best part of the book. But the final part of the book is a post script written by a character, John who is a novelist, and the post script ruined the mystery for me and seemed like an easy way out for Gaarder. (That's all I can say without spoilers).<br /><br />So, I rated this book 3 stars. It doesn't get stars for the storyline, the beginning was slow and the big mystery seemed ruined for me at the end, but it does get stars for Gaarder's articulate descriptions of philosophical biology and the scientific existentialism I think we've all felt at one point or another when we look up at the stars and realize how far away they are and how old they are, and how ephemeral we are in comparison. </span>
  <a data-text-id="824430706638284334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570601764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570601764" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6638317-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368732701p2/6638317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570601764?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/6638317-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570601764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1337512779182152069">I'm not sure of my ultimate ideas on fate, but I absolutely believe that some books are meant to be read at specific times in a specific person's life. <br/>But you know when you start a book and you're kind of exasperated & frustrated with it, and the back of your mind starts pushing an idea to the front: "Switch books...switch books!"? Sometimes, if you keep reading, it'll turn out that you were meant to read it, <i>right then, right now</i>. And that was my experience with <u>Maya</u>.<br/><br/>Talk about a slow start --</span>
  <span id="freeText1337512779182152069" style="display:none">I'm not sure of my ultimate ideas on fate, but I absolutely believe that some books are meant to be read at specific times in a specific person's life. <br />But you know when you start a book and you're kind of exasperated &amp; frustrated with it, and the back of your mind starts pushing an idea to the front: "Switch books...switch books!"? Sometimes, if you keep reading, it'll turn out that you were meant to read it, <i>right then, right now</i>. And that was my experience with <u>Maya</u>.<br /><br />Talk about a slow start -- the action, the really interesting action, doesn't start until about 2/3 through. Even then, it's introduced and described inexpertly -- as pure exposition. And it's only when John Spooke's postscript begins that you realize Gaarder has some incredibly complex ideas about artifice, illusion, and what constitutes personal reality. <br /><br />The characters aren't overly exciting (and many are grating &amp; pretentious), the plot is sort of banal (especially while they're still on the island), there is bordering-on-excessive scientific conversation --- but the merit's in the grand themes and how all is drawn together at the end. <u>Maya</u> reminded me quite sharply of how important, powerful, art is -- and of the meaning we ascribe to it without really understanding why.<br /><br /><br />Goodreads, let's get some half stars going, please?! Jesus. This is a solid 3.5 stars. I'd give it a shot if you need an escapist novel with a little substance -- and don't mind imaginary conversations with geckoes. No, seriously, those were among my favourite parts, though!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1337512779182152069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1042241533?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raisa" href="/user/show/11420292-raisa">Raisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1042241533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15901960146200762012">I picked this book up purely on the strength of Sophie's World, which was a great read. Sadly, Maya didn't pack a punch like Sophie's World did.<br/><br/>In Maya, a group of people in Taveuni in Fiji meet and connect. The narrator, a lonely Norwegian is intrigued by a Spanish couple who go around spouting mysterious aphorisms. The whole group connect in different ways and discuss life, love and other things. <br/><br/>For me, the first half of the book was extremely slow moving. Particularly a section which had the</span>
  <span id="freeText15901960146200762012" style="display:none">I picked this book up purely on the strength of Sophie's World, which was a great read. Sadly, Maya didn't pack a punch like Sophie's World did.<br /><br />In Maya, a group of people in Taveuni in Fiji meet and connect. The narrator, a lonely Norwegian is intrigued by a Spanish couple who go around spouting mysterious aphorisms. The whole group connect in different ways and discuss life, love and other things. <br /><br />For me, the first half of the book was extremely slow moving. Particularly a section which had the Norwegian holding a long philosophical conversation with... a gecko. A gecko named Gordon. <br /><br />In Sophie's World, I had this feeling that the story was 2D, just something to illustrate the philosophical concepts. And then the twist in the end just made the whole story stronger. I was sort of expecting a twist with Maya too- but that never really came. <br /><br />Granted, the last bit of the book was much faster paced and interesting, with a bit of magic and mystery moving things along. In the end, I feel like there wasn't a real climactic point, story-wise. <br /><br />However, Maya did raise some tough questions about life, and what it really means to be human. It causes you to realise, once again, what a vast, magical universe we live in, and how incredible it is that we're here at all. So, the book did have some mind-blowing moments. But the story lacked some punch, which let the whole thing down. I'd give Maya two and a half stars, for the mindblowing bits alone. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15901960146200762012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81425074?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/1176067-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1176067-jackie?shelf=2009-75-book-challenge">2009-75-book-challenge</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81425074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2904595859840067704">This is my first Gaarder book, as well as my first “philosophy” book. Admittedly, during the first portion of the book I had a hard time getting into it. There is quite a bit of mystery built up and much talk of evolution as the main character is a biologist. The dialogue is designed to make the reader question their own beliefs and purpose in life, which I thought was very interesting. However, the evolutionary discussions at times became too heavy and tiresome to read. It seemed like Frank, th</span>
  <span id="freeText2904595859840067704" style="display:none">This is my first Gaarder book, as well as my first “philosophy” book. Admittedly, during the first portion of the book I had a hard time getting into it. There is quite a bit of mystery built up and much talk of evolution as the main character is a biologist. The dialogue is designed to make the reader question their own beliefs and purpose in life, which I thought was very interesting. However, the evolutionary discussions at times became too heavy and tiresome to read. It seemed like Frank, the main character, would constantly digress into an evolutionary speech whenever given a chance and at times he came off as very whiney as he went on and on. However, once I stopped focusing on these digressions I started to enjoy the story itself, which come to find out is in fact a love story despite all the evolutionary discussions. <br /><br />What I found most surprising was that I didn’t like any of the characters, but despite this I loved the message and thought the concept of the book was awesome. Weird, as I never had this experience before with a book that at times I felt like I had to push myself to get through. I’m not sure if I would pick up another Gaarder book anytime soon since it seems like I would have to be in the frame of mind to read another philosophy book like this but I definitely would not discount him in the future. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2904595859840067704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer6175519052142370320">Probably the most philosophical book I have read from Gaarder, and that is saying a lot. The first half of the book takes place at a “tropical summit” where issues ranging from evolution, the universe, and the meaning of consciousness. The second half of the book moves into a mystery and explores the issues of love and of loss. I don’t think there was an issue that Gaarder did not touch in this book. Needless to say, it made for some interesting conversations with myself and had me explore my ow</span>
  <span id="freeText6175519052142370320" style="display:none">Probably the most philosophical book I have read from Gaarder, and that is saying a lot. The first half of the book takes place at a “tropical summit” where issues ranging from evolution, the universe, and the meaning of consciousness. The second half of the book moves into a mystery and explores the issues of love and of loss. I don’t think there was an issue that Gaarder did not touch in this book. Needless to say, it made for some interesting conversations with myself and had me explore my own beliefs and thoughts on the many issues.<br /><br />I don’t think I would have appreciated this book as much as I did if I hadn’t read Gaarder’s other books. The Joker from the Solitaire Mystery even plays a large role in the latter half of the book that if I hadn’t read it before, I may have been more confused than I already was. <br /><br />Those interested in philosophy would really enjoy this book. Gaarder is by far my favorite author, but I would not recommend this book to you if this is your first of his stories. <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8100459402317716651">Finished this a while ago, but been putting off a review because I'm not quite sure what to write.<br/><br/>It's difficult to review a Gaarder book without referring to the other Gaarder books you've read. Sophie's World blew me away. I love the Solitaire Mystery possibly more. The Christmas Mystery was so-so. So I wasn't sure what to expect from this one.<br/><br/>I'm not quite sure what I got either.<br/><br/>Maya is either insane or brilliant. It's definitely "Gaarder".   <span id="freeText8100459402317716651" style="display:none">Finished this a while ago, but been putting off a review because I'm not quite sure what to write.<br /><br />It's difficult to review a Gaarder book without referring to the other Gaarder books you've read. Sophie's World blew me away. I love the Solitaire Mystery possibly more. The Christmas Mystery was so-so. So I wasn't sure what to expect from this one.<br /><br />I'm not quite sure what I got either.<br /><br />Maya is either insane or brilliant. It's definitely "Gaarder". If you don't know what that means, I'd actually recommend reading the Solitaire Mystery first, for various reasons. And if you do know what I mean, but haven't Solitaire yet, I'd recommend reading it first anyway.<br /><br />In short, and without giving anything away, if you like mysteries inside mysteries, and don't mind feeling lost and a bit dizzy, definitely give it a go. It took me maybe a third of the book to get into it properly, but by the end it was a 5-starrer.</span>

  <span id="freeText13058629961853908741" style="display:none">One of the strangest reading experiences I've had in a long time. This book is a mystery inside a mystery - a whole other book inside a book. I wasn't prepared for the questions raised in this book and neither was I prepared for this style of writing, and many times I had to stop and reread parts. <br />I finished this book in two days, when I should have been studying for an exam.<br />The first 100 pages or so do not make sense until you get to to the second half of the book, at which point you find yourself getting sucked into the way the characters are related to each other. <br />My only problem is - the story is somewhat loose towards the ending. What about that element of magic that was introduced towards the end of the letter? When I started to read Spooke's postscript afterwards, I felt it was so mundane compared to the air that had been built up so far in the book. <br />I would most probably read it again - which is not something I can say for every book.</span>

<div id="review_1457703380" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1457703380" />
    <a title="Lee Kofman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37486301-lee-kofman"><img alt="Lee Kofman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560237201p2/37486301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1457703380?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Kofman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Kofman" href="/user/show/37486301-lee-kofman">Lee Kofman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1457703380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10577087080490013770">I really loved the first 50 or so pages of Maya, despite the utterly depressing subject matter (acute dread of death). However, once Gaarder introduced a speaking lizard into the book, it went downhill for me. I found the dialogue and plot to be often forced at the service of conveying ideas which were interesting (e.g. different approaches to coping with death and to understanding the creation of our universe, and the probability of existence of some divinity), however overwritten, repetitive. </span>
  <span id="freeText10577087080490013770" style="display:none">I really loved the first 50 or so pages of Maya, despite the utterly depressing subject matter (acute dread of death). However, once Gaarder introduced a speaking lizard into the book, it went downhill for me. I found the dialogue and plot to be often forced at the service of conveying ideas which were interesting (e.g. different approaches to coping with death and to understanding the creation of our universe, and the probability of existence of some divinity), however overwritten, repetitive. The plot which began as such a riveting mystery eventually deteriorated into something contrived and not satisfying (either I lost the plot too… or the writer ‘forgot’ to tie many loose ends). Still, I don’t feel like I wasted time reading this book, as it made me think again about the really big questions of existence – in the face of death, is it worth living? Can we ever understand why we are here? Light stuff like that… </span>
  <a data-text-id="10577087080490013770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1282823609?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana Mladenović" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana Mladenović" href="/user/show/35775435-ana-mladenovi">Ana Mladenović</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1282823609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8572348864913519487">To be honest with you, this is a Jostein Gaarder book, so I was going to either be blown away by it or, at the very least, mildly like it. Unfortunately, my opinion seems to fall into the second cathegory and it's greatly influenced solely by appreciation of the pure existence of this writer. As always, the writing was impeccable. I truly admire Gaarder's dedication to accuracy of the facts, as well as wonderfully put details and character quirks. With that in mind, and as typical as this sounds</span>
  <span id="freeText8572348864913519487" style="display:none">To be honest with you, this is a Jostein Gaarder book, so I was going to either be blown away by it or, at the very least, mildly like it. Unfortunately, my opinion seems to fall into the second cathegory and it's greatly influenced solely by appreciation of the pure existence of this writer. As always, the writing was impeccable. I truly admire Gaarder's dedication to accuracy of the facts, as well as wonderfully put details and character quirks. With that in mind, and as typical as this sounds, it missed a little "something" that usually makes me shiver when I read other books of his. I thought the ending seemed way too rushed. But wow, the Manifesto is brilliant, I was smittened by the actual beauty of some sentences. <br />The overall impression is a solid 3, and I hope I'm not being too biased here.</span>

          </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30079754-octavia-cade?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1788456519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8174904782762778731">The book to which this is a sequel, <i>The Solitaire Mystery</i>, is one of my favourites. This, sad to say, is not. There are some interesting bits here - I particularly love the idea of a woman who looks exactly like the subject of two Goya paintings - but they are buried very deep down. <i>Maya</i> starts to gather momentum about 200 pages in, when the protagonist goes to visit the Prado, but it's a slog to get that far. The Fijian "summit" meeting is just plain dull. (Pompous and pontificating may be a be</span>
  <span id="freeText8174904782762778731" style="display:none">The book to which this is a sequel, <i>The Solitaire Mystery</i>, is one of my favourites. This, sad to say, is not. There are some interesting bits here - I particularly love the idea of a woman who looks exactly like the subject of two Goya paintings - but they are buried very deep down. <i>Maya</i> starts to gather momentum about 200 pages in, when the protagonist goes to visit the Prado, but it's a slog to get that far. The Fijian "summit" meeting is just plain dull. (Pompous and pontificating may be a better description.) I remember reading <i>Sophie's World</i> and being amazed at the ingenious ways in which Gaarder wrote about philosophy. He does not have the same gift for writing about science, and this book, though it gets an extra point for intelligence, is woefully self-indulgent and flabby about the edges. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8174904782762778731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45386126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3519328894892184013">I am (very) slowly working my way through Gaarder's works and I continue to enjoy the process. My feelings about this book are quite contradictory: sometimes I felt it was a masterpiece, sometimes I felt it was very flawed. The story itself is enjoyable enough and vividly painted by Gaarder. The ideas it presents are captivating to me and beautifully worded. However, it is overly repetitive and I felt quite let down by the 'postscript.' On the whole, though, it was a good read and I'd recommend </span>
  <span id="freeText3519328894892184013" style="display:none">I am (very) slowly working my way through Gaarder's works and I continue to enjoy the process. My feelings about this book are quite contradictory: sometimes I felt it was a masterpiece, sometimes I felt it was very flawed. The story itself is enjoyable enough and vividly painted by Gaarder. The ideas it presents are captivating to me and beautifully worded. However, it is overly repetitive and I felt quite let down by the 'postscript.' On the whole, though, it was a good read and I'd recommend it to fans of Gaarder's other books. The translation by James Anderson is excellent.</span>

The many discussions about the meaning of existence just came across as pompous. And the ending was pretty disappointing. Can't really recommend this one.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('t88vDO+cYmeWhO2R0ScXglfQ5Wo0e7Mgx52Q+84ULtHM9v7Taj+CbGe9jEsGzFe2+j0pK6NFbEyJFem6e/42Tw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

The illusion seems part political hocus-pocus and also part wishful thinking, for among many Russians Stalin is again popular; the bloody dictator can boast a two-to-one approval rating. Decidedly better than that of Renko, whose lover, Eva, has left him for Detective Nikolai Isakov, a charismatic veteran of the civil war in Chechnya, a hero of the far right and, Renko suspects, a killer for hire. The cases entwine, and Renko’s quests become a personal inquiry fueled by jealousy.The investigation leads to the fields of Tver outside of Moscow, where once a million soldiers fought. There, amidst the detritus, Renko must confront the ghost of his own father, a favorite general of Stalin’s. In these barren fields, patriots and shady entrepreneurs—the Red Diggers and Black Diggers—collect the bones, weapons and personal effects of slain World War II soldiers, and find that even among the dead there are surprises.Stalin’s Ghost is replete with Martin Cruz Smith’s trademark wit, dark humor and action. In this tale of Arkady Renko, Smith has again fashioned an unforgettable character as cynical as Philip Marlowe, but with the heart of a Chekhovian Everyman. Since then she\'s kept her hands full and her mind and heart occupied by running The Sewing Circus, an all-girl group she founded to provide badly needed services like day care and job counseling to young women, many of whom are single mothers. More important, The Circus is a place for lively, wide-ranging, heart-to-heart discussions that will help members grow into what Joyce likes to call &quot;twenty-first-century free women.&quot; \n\nAll in all, Joyce has a full and rich life. She has her work, her family, her friends, and her town. But there are some nights when she crawls into bed alone and has to admit that something is missing. What she doesn\'t have is that red dress she keeps dreaming about or a social life that would accommodate it even if she braved the mall and bought one. To further complicate matters, she may not have The Sewing Circus much longer, as the state legislature has decided not to fund the group\'s vital but hard to define work with young women who are too often regarded as problems rather than possibilities. \n\nFeeling defeated and pessimistic, Joyce reluctantly agrees to keep a date for dinner at the home of her best friend, Sister -- a reverend like no other-and finds not only a perfect meal but a tall, dark stranger named Nate Anderson. Nate has just joined the administration at the high school and his unexpected presence in Idlewild touches a chord in Joyce that she thought her heart had forgotten how to play. Nate feels the same immediate connection, but both have enough experience with broken hearts to take it real slow. Besides, they\'ve got too much work to do to concentrate on falling in love....\n\nBut life moves at its own pace, and as Sister says, &quot;if you want to make God laugh, make plans.&quot; Particularly when it comes to matters of the heart. Joyce decides the trick is to stay focused and to remember that nothing is as sexy as the truth, the whole truth, and nothing but the truth, especially if you tell it while you\'re wearing a perfect red dress...<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8490923275315057314\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13086521915603077008">Jostein Gaarder is a Norwegian intellectual and author of several novels, short stories, and children's books. Gaarder often writes from the perspective of children, exploring their sense of wonder about the world. He often uses meta-fiction in his works, writing stories within stories.<br/><br/>Gaarder was born into a pedagogical family. His best known work is the novel <i>Sophie's World</i>, subtitled "A Novel a</span>
  <span id="freeText13086521915603077008" style="display:none">Jostein Gaarder is a Norwegian intellectual and author of several novels, short stories, and children's books. Gaarder often writes from the perspective of children, exploring their sense of wonder about the world. He often uses meta-fiction in his works, writing stories within stories.<br /><br />Gaarder was born into a pedagogical family. His best known work is the novel <i>Sophie's World</i>, subtitled "A Novel about the History of Philosophy." This popular work has been translated into fifty-three languages; there are over thirty million copies in print, with three million copies sold in Germany alone.<br /><br />In 1997, he established the Sophie Prize together with his wife Siri Dannevig. This prize is an international environment and development prize (USD 100,000 = 77,000 €), awarded annually. It is named after the novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13086521915603077008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/215160-maya">Quotes from Maya</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It takes billions of years to create a human being. And it takes only a few seconds to die.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/367870-it-takes-billions-of-years-to-create-a-human-being">149 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;When we die, as when the scenes have been fixed on to celluloid and the scenery is pulled down and burnt — we are phantoms in the memories of our descendants. Then we are ghosts, my dear, then we are myths. But still we are together. We are the past together, we are a distant past. Beneath the dome of the mysterious stars, I still hear your voice.&rdquo;</span>
